<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">In geometry, study of the relationship between angles and lengths</div>

<p><b>Trigonometry</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%84%CF%81%CE%AF%CE%B3%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:τρίγωνον">τρίγωνον</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>trígōnon</i></span>)</i> 'triangle', and <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BC%CE%AD%CF%84%CF%81%CE%BF%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:μέτρον">μέτρον</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>métron</i></span>)</i> 'measure')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> that studies relationships between side lengths and <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">angles</a> of <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">triangles</a>. The field emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic world</a> during the 3rd century BC from applications of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a> to <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomical studies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The Greeks focused on the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_table_of_chords" title="Ptolemy's table of chords">calculation of chords</a>, while mathematicians in India created the earliest-known tables of values for  trigonometric ratios (also called <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_functions" title="Trigonometric functions">trigonometric functions</a>) such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_3-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-3'>[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout history, trigonometry has been applied in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Geodesy" title="Geodesy">geodesy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveying</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">celestial mechanics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hackley1853_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hackley1853-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trigonometry is known for its many <a href="/wiki/Identity_(mathematics)" title="Identity (mathematics)">identities</a>. These 
<a href="/wiki/List_of_trigonometric_identities" title="List of trigonometric identities">trigonometric identities</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sterling2014_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sterling2014-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halmos2013_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halmos2013-6">[6]</a></sup> are commonly used for rewriting trigonometrical <a href="/wiki/Expression_(mathematics)" title="Expression (mathematics)">expressions</a> with the aim to simplify an expression, to find a more useful form of an expression, or to <a href="/wiki/Equation_solving" title="Equation solving">solve an equation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LarsonHostetler2006_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-LarsonHostetler2006-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_trigonometry" title="History of trigonometry">History of trigonometry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hipparchos_1.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="268" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Hipparchos_1.jpeg/180px-Hipparchos_1.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/Hipparchos_1.jpeg 1.5x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hipparchos_1.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a>, credited with compiling the first <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_tables" title="Trigonometric tables">trigonometric table</a>, has been described as "the father of trigonometry".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage162_162]"Greek_Trigonometry_and_Mensuration"_8-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage162_162]"Greek_Trigonometry_and_Mensuration"-8'>[8]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> astronomers studied angle measure, using a division of circles into 360 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> They, and later the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonians" title="Babylonians">Babylonians</a>, studied the ratios of the sides of <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similar</a> triangles and discovered some properties of these ratios but did not turn that into a systematic method for finding sides and angles of triangles. The <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">ancient Nubians</a> used a similar method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 3rd century BC, <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Hellenistic mathematicians</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> studied the properties of <a href="/wiki/Chord_(geometry)" title="Chord (geometry)">chords</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inscribed_angle" title="Inscribed angle">inscribed angles</a> in circles, and they proved theorems that are equivalent to modern trigonometric formulae, although they presented them geometrically rather than algebraically. In 140 BC, <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a>, Asia Minor) gave the first tables of chords, analogous to modern <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_tables" title="Trigonometric tables">tables of sine values</a>, and used them to solve problems in trigonometry and <a href="/wiki/Spherical_trigonometry" title="Spherical trigonometry">spherical trigonometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThurston1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrNpHjqxQQ9oCpgPA235vonepageqffalse_235–236]&quot;Appendix_1:_Hipparchus's_Table_of_Chords&quot;_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThurston1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrNpHjqxQQ9oCpgPA235vonepageqffalse_235–236]&quot;Appendix_1:_Hipparchus's_Table_of_Chords&quot;-11">[11]</a></sup> In the 2nd century AD, the Greco-Egyptian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (from Alexandria, Egypt) constructed detailed trigonometric tables (<a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_table_of_chords" title="Ptolemy's table of chords">Ptolemy's table of chords</a>) in Book 1, chapter 11 of his <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toomer_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-toomer-12">[12]</a></sup> Ptolemy used <a href="/wiki/Chord_(geometry)" title="Chord (geometry)">chord</a> length to define his trigonometric functions, a minor difference from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a> convention we use today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThurston1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrNpHjqxQQ9oCpgPA239vonepageqffalse_239–243]&quot;Appendix_3:_Ptolemy's_Table_of_Chords&quot;_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThurston1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrNpHjqxQQ9oCpgPA239vonepageqffalse_239–243]&quot;Appendix_3:_Ptolemy's_Table_of_Chords&quot;-13">[13]</a></sup> (The value we call sin(θ) can be found by looking up the chord length for twice the angle of interest (2θ) in Ptolemy's table, and then dividing that value by two.) Centuries passed before more detailed tables were produced, and Ptolemy's treatise remained in use for performing trigonometric calculations in astronomy throughout the next 1200 years in the medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic</a>, and, later, Western European worlds.
</p><p>The modern sine convention is first attested in the <i><a href="/wiki/Surya_Siddhanta" title="Surya Siddhanta">Surya Siddhanta</a></i>, and its properties were further documented by the 5th century (AD) <a href="/wiki/Indian_mathematics" title="Indian mathematics">Indian mathematician</a> and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991215_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991215-14">[14]</a></sup> These Greek and Indian works were translated and expanded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">medieval Islamic mathematicians</a>. By the 10th century, Islamic mathematicians were using all six trigonometric functions, had tabulated their values, and were applying them to problems in <a href="/wiki/Spherical_geometry" title="Spherical geometry">spherical geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willers2018_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willers2018-16">[16]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Nasir al-Din al-Tusi</a> has been described as the creator of trigonometry as a mathematical discipline in its own right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> He was the first to treat trigonometry as a mathematical discipline independent from astronomy, and he developed spherical trigonometry into its present form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-20">[20]</a></sup> He listed the six distinct cases of a right-angled triangle in spherical trigonometry, and in his <i>On the Sector Figure</i>, he stated the law of sines for plane and spherical triangles, discovered the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_tangents" title="Law of tangents">law of tangents</a> for spherical triangles, and provided proofs for both these laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Knowledge of trigonometric functions and methods reached <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> via <a href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_the_12th_century" title="Latin translations of the 12th century">Latin translations</a> of Ptolemy's Greek <i>Almagest</i> as well as the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">Persian and Arab astronomers</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_J%C4%81bir_al-Harr%C4%81n%C4%AB_al-Batt%C4%81n%C4%AB" title="Muhammad ibn Jābir al-Harrānī al-Battānī">Al Battani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Nasir al-Din al-Tusi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991237,_274_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991237,_274-22">[22]</a></sup> One of the earliest works on trigonometry by a northern European mathematician is <i>De Triangulis</i> by the 15th century German mathematician <a href="/wiki/Regiomontanus" title="Regiomontanus">Regiomontanus</a>, who was encouraged to write, and provided with a copy of the <i>Almagest</i>, by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_scholars_in_Renaissance" title="Byzantine scholars in Renaissance">Byzantine Greek scholar</a> cardinal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilios_Bessarion" title="Basilios Bessarion">Basilios Bessarion</a> with whom he lived for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> At the same time, another translation of the <i>Almagest</i> from Greek into Latin was completed by the Cretan <a href="/wiki/George_of_Trebizond" title="George of Trebizond">George of Trebizond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Trigonometry was still so little known in 16th-century northern Europe that <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> devoted two chapters of <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> to explain its basic concepts.
</p><p>Driven by the demands of <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a> and the growing need for accurate maps of large geographic areas, trigonometry grew into a major branch of mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bartholomaeus_Pitiscus" title="Bartholomaeus Pitiscus">Bartholomaeus Pitiscus</a> was the first to use the word, publishing his <i>Trigonometria</i> in 1595.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gemma_Frisius" title="Gemma Frisius">Gemma Frisius</a> described for the first time the method of <a href="/wiki/Triangulation" title="Triangulation">triangulation</a> still used today in surveying. It was <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> who fully incorporated <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a> into trigonometry. The works of the Scottish mathematicians <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Gregory_(astronomer_and_mathematician)" title="James Gregory (astronomer and mathematician)">James Gregory</a> in the 17th century and <a href="/wiki/Colin_Maclaurin" title="Colin Maclaurin">Colin Maclaurin</a> in the 18th century were influential in the development of <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_series" title="Trigonometric series">trigonometric series</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Also in the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Brook_Taylor" title="Brook Taylor">Brook Taylor</a> defined the general <a href="/wiki/Taylor_series" title="Taylor series">Taylor series</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trigonometric_ratios">Trigonometric ratios</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonometric_function" title="Trigonometric function">Trigonometric function</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trigonometry_triangle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Trigonometry_triangle.svg/220px-Trigonometry_triangle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Trigonometry_triangle.svg/330px-Trigonometry_triangle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Trigonometry_triangle.svg/440px-Trigonometry_triangle.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trigonometry_triangle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In this right triangle: <span class="texhtml">sin <i>A</i> = <i>a</i>/<i>h</i>;</span> <span class="texhtml">cos <i>A</i> = <i>b</i>/<i>h</i>;</span> <span class="texhtml">tan <i>A</i> = <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>.</span></div></div></div>
<p>Trigonometric ratios are the ratios between edges of a right triangle. These ratios are given by the following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonometric_function" title="Trigonometric function">trigonometric functions</a> of the known angle <i>A</i>, where <i>a</i>, <i> b</i> and <i>h</i> refer to the lengths of the sides in the accompanying figure:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">Sine</a></b> function (sin), defined as the ratio of the side opposite the angle to the <a href="/wiki/Hypotenuse" title="Hypotenuse">hypotenuse</a>.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \sin A={\frac {\textrm {opposite}}{\textrm {hypotenuse}}}={\frac {a}{h}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">Cosine</a></b> function (cos), defined as the ratio of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adjacent_side_(right_triangle)" title="Adjacent side (right triangle)">adjacent</a> leg (the side of the triangle joining the angle to the right angle) to the hypotenuse.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \cos A={\frac {\textrm {adjacent}}{\textrm {hypotenuse}}}={\frac {b}{h}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangent_(trigonometric_function)" title="Tangent (trigonometric function)">Tangent</a></b> function (tan), defined as the ratio of the opposite leg to the adjacent leg.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tan A={\frac {\textrm {opposite}}{\textrm {adjacent}}}={\frac {a}{b}}={\frac {a/h}{b/h}}={\frac {\sin A}{\cos A}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \tan A={\frac {\textrm {opposite}}{\textrm {adjacent}}}={\frac {a}{b}}={\frac {a/h}{b/h}}={\frac {\sin A}{\cos A}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \tan A={\frac {\textrm {opposite}}{\textrm {adjacent}}}={\frac {a}{b}}={\frac {a/h}{b/h}}={\frac {\sin A}{\cos A}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e53008722bca1d56dfa2b0ae96e91ab7949e1396" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:40.669ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hypotenuse" title="Hypotenuse">hypotenuse</a> is the side opposite to the 90 degree angle in a right triangle; it is the longest side of the triangle and one of the two sides adjacent to angle <i>A</i>. The <b>adjacent leg</b> is the other side that is adjacent to angle <i>A</i>. The <b>opposite side</b> is the side that is opposite to angle <i>A</i>. The terms <b>perpendicular</b> and <b>base</b> are sometimes used for the opposite and adjacent sides respectively. See below under <a href="#Mnemonics">Mnemonics</a>.
</p><p>Since any two right triangles with the same acute angle <i>A</i> are <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similar</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRedlin2015_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRedlin2015-29">[29]</a></sup> the value of a trigonometric ratio depends only on the angle <i>A</i>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse" title="Multiplicative inverse">reciprocals</a> of these functions are named the <b>cosecant</b> (csc), <b>secant</b> (sec), and <b>cotangent</b> (cot), respectively:
</p>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\cot A={\frac  {1}{\tan A}}={\frac  {{\textrm  {adjacent}}}{{\textrm  {opposite}}}}={\frac  {\cos A}{\sin A}}={\frac  {b}{a}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/087f418e780f9d7adac7c4c9f90e241f60838b86" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:42.167ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The cosine, cotangent, and cosecant are so named because they are respectively the sine, tangent, and secant of the complementary angle abbreviated to "co-".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JardineShell-Gellasch2011_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-JardineShell-Gellasch2011-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>With these functions, one can answer virtually all questions about arbitrary triangles by using the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_sines" title="Law of sines">law of sines</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_cosines" title="Law of cosines">law of cosines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ForsethBurger2008_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-ForsethBurger2008-31">[31]</a></sup> These laws can be used to compute the remaining angles and sides of any triangle as soon as two sides and their included angle or two angles and a side or three sides are known.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mnemonics"><span class="anchor" id="SOHCAHTOA"></span>Mnemonics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mnemonics_in_trigonometry" title="Mnemonics in trigonometry">Mnemonics in trigonometry</a></div>
<p>A common use of <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">mnemonics</a> is to remember facts and relationships in trigonometry. For example, the <i>sine</i>, <i>cosine</i>, and <i>tangent</i> ratios in a right triangle can be remembered by representing them and their corresponding sides as strings of letters. For instance, a mnemonic is SOH-CAH-TOA:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><b>S</b>ine = <b>O</b>pposite ÷ <b>H</b>ypotenuse</dd>
<dd><b>C</b>osine = <b>A</b>djacent ÷ <b>H</b>ypotenuse</dd>
<dd><b>T</b>angent = <b>O</b>pposite ÷ <b>A</b>djacent</dd></dl>
<p>One way to remember the letters is to sound them out phonetically (i.e. <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">SOH</span>-kə-<span style="font-size:90%">TOH</span>-ə</i></a>, similar to <a href="/wiki/Krakatoa" title="Krakatoa">Krakatoa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Another method is to expand the letters into a sentence, such as "<b>S</b>ome <b>O</b>ld <b>H</b>ippie <b>C</b>aught <b>A</b>nother <b>H</b>ippie <b>T</b>rippin' <b>O</b>n <b>A</b>cid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_unit_circle_and_common_trigonometric_values">The unit circle and common trigonometric values</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">Unit circle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sin-cos-defn-I.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="705" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sin-cos-defn-I.png/220px-Sin-cos-defn-I.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sin-cos-defn-I.png/330px-Sin-cos-defn-I.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sin-cos-defn-I.png/440px-Sin-cos-defn-I.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sin-cos-defn-I.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fig. 1a – Sine and cosine of an angle θ defined using the unit circle</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg/220px-Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg/330px-Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg/440px-Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Math_Trigonometry_Unit_Circle_Rotation_Sign_Indication.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indication of the sign and amount of key angles according to rotation direction</div></div></div>
<p>Trigonometric ratios can also be represented using the <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">unit circle</a>, which is the circle of radius 1 centered at the origin in the plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenTheodore2009_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-CohenTheodore2009-35">[35]</a></sup> In this setting, the <a href="/wiki/Angle#Positive_and_negative_angles" title="Angle">terminal side</a> of an angle <i>A</i> placed in <a href="/wiki/Angle#Positive_and_negative_angles" title="Angle">standard position</a> will intersect the unit circle in a point (x,y), where  <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x=\cos A}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>A</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x=\cos A}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle x=\cos A}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9021d5618133c8ead42c34be2590995081e82b97" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:9.669ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle y=\sin A}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>A</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle y=\sin A}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle y=\sin A}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3dc7ecd9349fdcd4a0be674ed1c2517d2a062a21" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:9.24ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenTheodore2009_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-CohenTheodore2009-35">[35]</a></sup> This representation allows for the calculation of commonly found trigonometric values, such as those in the following table:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelley2002_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelley2002-36">[36]</a></sup>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trigonometric_functions_of_real_or_complex_variables">Trigonometric functions of real or complex variables</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonometric_function" title="Trigonometric function">Trigonometric function</a></div>
<p>Using the <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">unit circle</a>, one can extend the definitions of trigonometric ratios to all positive and negative arguments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olive2003_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olive2003-37">[37]</a></sup> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonometric_function" title="Trigonometric function">trigonometric function</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graphs_of_trigonometric_functions">Graphs of trigonometric functions</span></h3>
<p>The following table summarizes the properties of the graphs of the six main trigonometric functions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Attenborough2003_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Attenborough2003-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LarsonEdwards2008_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-LarsonEdwards2008-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Function
</th>
<th>Period
</th>
<th>Domain
</th>
<th>Range
</th>
<th>Graph
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>sine
</th>
<td><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 2\pi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 2\pi }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="2\pi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/73efd1f6493490b058097060a572606d2c550a06" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.494ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>
</td>
<td><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (-\infty ,\infty )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (-\infty ,\infty )}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="(-\infty ,\infty )" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0c8c11c44279888c9e395eeb5f45d121348ae10a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:9.299ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>
</td>
<td><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle [-1,1]}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inverse_trigonometric_functions">Inverse trigonometric functions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inverse_trigonometric_functions" title="Inverse trigonometric functions">Inverse trigonometric functions</a></div>
<p>Because the six main trigonometric functions are periodic, they are not <a href="/wiki/Injective_function" title="Injective function">injective</a> (or, 1 to 1), and thus are not invertible. By <a href="/wiki/Restriction_(mathematics)" title="Restriction (mathematics)">restricting</a> the domain of a trigonometric function, however, they can be made invertible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BremiganBremigan2011_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-BremiganBremigan2011-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 48ff">: 48ff </span></sup>
</p><p>The names of the inverse trigonometric functions, together with their domains and range, can be found in the following table:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BremiganBremigan2011_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-BremiganBremigan2011-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 48ff">: 48ff </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrokateManchanda2019_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrokateManchanda2019-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 521ff">: 521ff </span></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Name
</th>
<th>Usual notation
</th>
<th>Definition
</th>
<th>Domain of <i>x</i> for real result
</th>
<th>Range of usual principal value <br/> (<a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radians</a>)
</th>
<th>Range of usual principal value <br/> (<a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degrees</a>)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>arcsine</b></td>
<td><i>y</i> = <span class="texhtml">arcsin(<i>x</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> = <span class="texhtml"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sin</a>(<i>y</i>)</span></td>
<td>−1 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1</td>
<td>−<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> ≤ <i>y</i> ≤ <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span></td>
<td>−90° ≤ <i>y</i> ≤ 90°
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>arccosine</b></td>
<td><i>y</i> = <span class="texhtml">arccos(<i>x</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> = <span class="texhtml"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">cos</a>(<i>y</i>)</span></td>
<td>−1 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1</td>
<td>0 ≤ <i>y</i> ≤ <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></td>
<td>0° ≤ <i>y</i> ≤ 180°
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>arctangent</b></td>
<td><i>y</i> = <span class="texhtml">arctan(<i>x</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> = <span class="texhtml"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangent_(trigonometry)" title="Tangent (trigonometry)">tan</a>(<i>y</i>)</span></td>
<td>all real numbers</td>
<td>−<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> &lt; <i>y</i> &lt; <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span></td>
<td>−90° &lt; <i>y</i> &lt; 90°
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>arccotangent</b></td>
<td><i>y</i> = <span class="texhtml">arccot(<i>x</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> = <span class="texhtml"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cotangent" title="Cotangent">cot</a>(<i>y</i>)</span></td>
<td>all real numbers
</td>
<td>0 &lt; <i>y</i> &lt; <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></td>
<td>0° &lt; <i>y</i> &lt; 180°
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>arcsecant</b></td>
<td><i>y</i> = <span class="texhtml">arcsec(<i>x</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> = <span class="texhtml"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secant_(trigonometry)" title="Secant (trigonometry)">sec</a>(<i>y</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> ≤ −1 or 1 ≤ <i>x</i></td>
<td>0 ≤ <i>y</i> &lt; <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> or <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> &lt; <i>y</i> ≤ <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></td>
<td>0° ≤ <i>y</i> &lt; 90° or 90° &lt; <i>y</i> ≤ 180°
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>arccosecant</b></td>
<td><i>y</i> = <span class="texhtml">arccsc(<i>x</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> = <span class="texhtml"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosecant" title="Cosecant">csc</a>(<i>y</i>)</span></td>
<td><i>x</i> ≤ −1 or 1 ≤ <i>x</i></td>
<td>−<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> ≤ <i>y</i> &lt; 0 or 0 &lt; <i>y</i> ≤ <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span></td>
<td>−90° ≤ <i>y</i> &lt; 0° or 0° &lt; <i>y</i> ≤ 90°
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>

<p>With these definitions the trigonometric functions can be defined for <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alessio2015_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alessio2015-43">[43]</a></sup> When extended as functions of real or complex variables, the following <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_formula" title="Euler's formula">formula</a> holds for the complex exponential:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle e^{x+iy}=e^{x}(\cos y+i\sin y).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>This complex exponential function, written in terms of trigonometric functions, is particularly useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAJESWARIRAO2014_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAJESWARIRAO2014-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stillwell2010_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stillwell2010-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calculating_trigonometric_functions">Calculating trigonometric functions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_tables" title="Trigonometric tables">Trigonometric tables</a></div>
<p>Trigonometric functions were among the earliest uses for <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_table" title="Mathematical table">mathematical tables</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell-KellyCampbell-Kelly2003_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-KellyCampbell-Kelly2003-46">[46]</a></sup> Such tables were incorporated into mathematics textbooks and students were taught to look up values and how to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpolate" title="Interpolate">interpolate</a> between the values listed to get higher accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DonovanGimmestad1980_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-DonovanGimmestad1980-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slide_rule" title="Slide rule">Slide rules</a> had special scales for trigonometric functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Middlemiss1945_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Middlemiss1945-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Scientific_calculator" title="Scientific calculator">Scientific calculators</a> have buttons for calculating the main trigonometric functions (sin, cos, tan, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_formula" title="Euler's formula">cis</a> and their inverses).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Most allow a choice of angle measurement methods: <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degrees</a>, radians, and sometimes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gradians" title="Gradians">gradians</a>. Most computer <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming languages</a> provide function libraries that include the trigonometric functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Floating_point_unit" title="Floating point unit">floating point unit</a> hardware incorporated into the microprocessor chips used in most personal computers has built-in instructions for calculating trigonometric functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_trigonometric_functions">Other trigonometric functions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_functions#History" title="Trigonometric functions">Trigonometric functions § History</a></div>
<p>In addition to the six ratios listed earlier, there are additional trigonometric functions that were historically important, though seldom used today. These include the <a href="/wiki/Chord_(geometry)" title="Chord (geometry)">chord</a> (<span class="texhtml">crd(<i>θ</i>) = 2 sin(<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>θ</i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>)</span>), the <a href="/wiki/Versine" title="Versine">versine</a> (<span class="texhtml">versin(<i>θ</i>) = 1 − cos(<i>θ</i>) = 2 sin<sup>2</sup>(<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>θ</i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>)</span>) (which appeared in the earliest tables<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991xxiii–xxiv_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991xxiii–xxiv-52">[52]</a></sup>), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coversine" title="Coversine">coversine</a> (<span class="texhtml">coversin(<i>θ</i>) = 1 − sin(<i>θ</i>) = versin(<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> − <i>θ</i>)</span>), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haversine" title="Haversine">haversine</a> (<span class="texhtml">haversin(<i>θ</i>) = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>versin(<i>θ</i>) = sin<sup>2</sup>(<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>θ</i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>)</span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENielsen1966xxiii–xxiv_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENielsen1966xxiii–xxiv-53">[53]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Exsecant" title="Exsecant">exsecant</a> (<span class="texhtml">exsec(<i>θ</i>) = sec(<i>θ</i>) − 1</span>), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excosecant" title="Excosecant">excosecant</a> (<span class="texhtml">excsc(<i>θ</i>) = exsec(<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> − <i>θ</i>) = csc(<i>θ</i>) − 1</span>). See <a href="/wiki/List_of_trigonometric_identities" title="List of trigonometric identities">List of trigonometric identities</a> for more relations between these functions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uses_of_trigonometry" title="Uses of trigonometry">Uses of trigonometry</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">Astronomy</a></div>
<p>For centuries, spherical trigonometry has been used for locating solar, lunar, and stellar positions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory1816_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory1816-54">[54]</a></sup> predicting eclipses, and describing the orbits of the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, the technique of <a href="/wiki/Triangulation" title="Triangulation">triangulation</a> is used in <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> to measure the distance to nearby stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeedsBackman2009_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-SeedsBackman2009-56">[56]</a></sup> as well as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_navigation_system" title="Satellite navigation system">satellite navigation systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willers2018_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Willers2018-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation">Navigation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">Navigation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg/220px-Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg/330px-Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg/440px-Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frieberger_drum_marine_sextant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sextant" title="Sextant">Sextants</a> are used to measure the angle of the sun or stars with respect to the horizon. Using trigonometry and a <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">marine chronometer</a>, the position of the ship can be determined from such measurements.</div></div></div>
<p>Historically, trigonometry has been used for locating latitudes and longitudes of sailing vessels, plotting courses, and calculating distances during navigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabine1800_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sabine1800-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trigonometry is still used in navigation through such means as the <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">Global Positioning System</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autonomous_vehicle" title="Autonomous vehicle">autonomous vehicles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surveying">Surveying</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">Surveying</a></div>
<p>In land <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveying</a>, trigonometry is used in the calculation of lengths, areas, and relative angles between objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perkins1853_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perkins1853-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>On a larger scale, trigonometry is used in <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a> to measure distances between landmarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WithersLorimer2015_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-WithersLorimer2015-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periodic_functions">Periodic functions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier series</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">Fourier transform</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fourier_series_and_transform.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2b/Fourier_series_and_transform.gif" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle s(x)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>s</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle s(x)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="s(x)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/10f51eec88706f1a26ac7430dc71d92c15e71a6e" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.229ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> (in red) is a sum of six sine functions of different amplitudes and harmonically related frequencies. Their summation is called a Fourier series. The Fourier transform, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S(f)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>f</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S(f)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S(f)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/04cf5fa5e69ff1a6bde0d265f3c319c17d7cf62f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.587ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> (in blue), which depicts <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a> vs <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>, reveals the 6 frequencies (<i>at odd harmonics</i>) and their amplitudes (<i>1/odd number</i>).</div></div></div>
<p>The sine and cosine functions are fundamental to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Periodic_function" title="Periodic function">periodic functions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorscheBerg2003_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-MorscheBerg2003-61">[61]</a></sup> such as those that describe sound and <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> waves. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Joseph_Fourier" title="Jean-Baptiste Joseph Fourier">Fourier</a> discovered that every <a href="/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">continuous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Periodic_function" title="Periodic function">periodic function</a> could be described as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infinite_series" title="Infinite series">infinite sum</a> of trigonometric functions.
</p><p>Even non-periodic functions can be represented as an <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">integral</a> of sines and cosines through the <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">Fourier transform</a>. This has applications to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thaller2007_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thaller2007-62">[62]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">communications</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rahman2011_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rahman2011-63">[63]</a></sup> among other fields.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics_and_acoustics">Optics and acoustics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a></div>
<p>Trigonometry is useful in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_science" title="Physical science">physical sciences</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BornsteinInc1966_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-BornsteinInc1966-64">[64]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchillerWurster1988_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchillerWurster1988-65">[65]</a></sup>  and <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchillerWurster1988_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchillerWurster1988-65">[65]</a></sup> In these areas, they are used to describe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_waves" title="Sound waves">sound</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_wave" title="Light wave">light waves</a>, and to solve boundary- and transmission-related problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Towne2014_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Towne2014-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_applications">Other applications</span></h3>
<p>Other fields that use trigonometry or trigonometric functions include <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">music theory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeinemanTarwater1992_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeinemanTarwater1992-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geodesy" title="Geodesy">geodesy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_synthesis" title="Audio synthesis">audio synthesis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KahrsBrandenburg2006_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-KahrsBrandenburg2006-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foulder2019_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foulder2019-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">medical imaging</a> (<a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladd2014_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ladd2014-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> (and hence <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryptology" title="Cryptology">cryptology</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkhipovChubarikov2008_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArkhipovChubarikov2008-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchillerWurster1988_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-SchillerWurster1988-65">[65]</a></sup> <a title="Image compression">image compression</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malmkjaer2009_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malmkjaer2009-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dadkhah2011_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dadkhah2011-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_engineering" title="Mechanical engineering">mechanical engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil engineering</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeinemanTarwater1992_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-HeinemanTarwater1992-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Computer_graphics" title="Computer graphics">computer graphics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffith2012_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith2012-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeinemanTarwater1992_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-HeinemanTarwater1992-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crystallography" title="Crystallography">crystallography</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffin1841_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffin1841-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_development" title="Game development">game development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffith2012_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith2012-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Identities">Identities</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trigonometric_identities" title="List of trigonometric identities">List of trigonometric identities</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="898" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png/220px-Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png/330px-Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png/440px-Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triangle_ABC_with_Sides_a_b_c_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Triangle with sides <i>a</i>,<i>b</i>,<i>c</i> and respectively opposite angles <i>A</i>,<i>B</i>,<i>C</i></div></div></div>
<p>Trigonometry has been noted for its many identities, that is, equations that are true for all possible inputs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dugopolski2002_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dugopolski2002-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Identities involving only angles are known as <i>trigonometric identities</i>. Other equations, known as <i>triangle identities</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BOARD2015_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-BOARD2015-82">[82]</a></sup> relate both the sides and angles of a given triangle.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Triangle_identities">Triangle identities</span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Triangle_identities"></span><span class="anchor" id="Common_formulas"></span>
</p><p>In the following identities, <i>A</i>, <i>B</i> and <i>C</i> are the angles of a triangle and <i>a</i>, <i>b</i> and <i>c</i> are the lengths of sides of the triangle opposite the respective angles (as shown in the diagram).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_of_sines">Law of sines</span></h4>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Law_of_sines" title="Law of sines">law of sines</a></b> (also known as the "sine rule") for an arbitrary triangle states:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2010_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young2010-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_of_cosines">Law of cosines</span></h4>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Law_of_cosines" title="Law of cosines">law of cosines</a></b> (known as the cosine formula, or the "cos rule") is an extension of the Pythagorean theorem to arbitrary triangles:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2010_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young2010-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c^{2}=a^{2}+b^{2}-2ab\cos C,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_of_tangents">Law of tangents</span></h4>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Law_of_tangents" title="Law of tangents">law of tangents</a></b>, developed by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Vi%C3%A8te" title="François Viète">François Viète</a>, is an alternative to the Law of Cosines when solving for the unknown edges of a triangle, providing simpler computations when using trigonometric tables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2010_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2010-85">[85]</a></sup> It is given by:

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Area">Area</span></h4>
<p>Given two sides <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> and the angle between the sides <i>C</i>, the area of the triangle is given by half the product of the lengths of two sides and the sine of the angle between the two sides:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2010_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Young2010-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heron%27s_formula" title="Heron's formula">Heron's formula</a> is another method that may be used to calculate the area of a triangle. This formula states that if a triangle has sides of lengths <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i>, and if the semiperimeter is
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle s={\frac {1}{2}}(a+b+c),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>s</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle s={\frac {1}{2}}(a+b+c),}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="s=\frac{1}{2}(a+b+c)," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bb6700e4d84c028818eab0c624626fd723c18d24" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:17.559ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>then the area of the triangle is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AufmannBarker2007_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-AufmannBarker2007-86">[86]</a></sup>

<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mbox{Area}}=\Delta ={\sqrt {s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}}={\frac {abc}{4R}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h4><span id="Euler.27s_formula"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Euler's_formula">Euler's formula</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_formula" title="Euler's formula">Euler's formula</a>, which states that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle e^{ix}=\cos x+i\sin x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\sin x = \frac{e^{ix} - e^{-ix}}{2i}, \qquad \cos x = \frac{e^{ix} + e^{-ix}}{2}, \qquad \tan x = \frac{i(e^{-ix} - e^{ix})}{e^{ix} + e^{-ix}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5b30e164c54b1788e201501d9e510a16e1a4921b" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:70.195ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_trigonometric_identities">Other trigonometric identities</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trigonometric_identities" title="List of trigonometric identities">List of trigonometric identities</a></div>
<p>Other commonly used trigonometric identities include the half-angle identities, the angle sum and difference identities, and the product-to-sum identities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRedlin2015_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRedlin2015-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://mathshistory.st-andrews.ac.uk/Biographies/Al-Tusi_Nasir/" rel="nofollow">"Nasir al-Din al-Tusi"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/MacTutor_History_of_Mathematics_archive" title="MacTutor History of Mathematics archive">MacTutor History of Mathematics archive</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2021-01-08</span></span>. <q>One of al-Tusi's most important mathematical contributions was the creation of trigonometry as a mathematical discipline in its own right rather than as just a tool for astronomical applications. In Treatise on the quadrilateral al-Tusi gave the first extant exposition of the whole system of plane and spherical trigonometry. This work is really the first in history on trigonometry as an independent branch of pure mathematics and the first in which all six cases for a right-angled spherical triangle are set forth.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=MacTutor+History+of+Mathematics+archive&amp;rft.atitle=Nasir+al-Din+al-Tusi&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fmathshistory.st-andrews.ac.uk%2FBiographies%2FAl-Tusi_Nasir%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATrigonometry"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBerggren2013">Berggren, J. L. (October 2013). "Islamic Mathematics". <a class="external text" href="https://www.cambridge.org/core/books/the-cambridge-history-of-science/islamic-mathematics/4BF4D143150C0013552902EE270AF9C2" rel="nofollow"><i>the cambridge history of science</i></a>. Vol. 2. Cambridge University Press. pp. 62–83. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1017%2FCHO9780511974007.004" rel="nofollow">10.1017/CHO9780511974007.004</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780521594486" title="Special:BookSources/9780521594486"><bdi>9780521594486</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Islamic+Mathematics&amp;rft.btitle=the+cambridge+history+of+science&amp;rft.pages=62-83&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2013-10&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2FCHO9780511974007.004&amp;rft.isbn=9780521594486&amp;rft.aulast=Berggren&amp;rft.aufirst=J.+L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cambridge.org%2Fcore%2Fbooks%2Fthe-cambridge-history-of-science%2Fislamic-mathematics%2F4BF4D143150C0013552902EE270AF9C2&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATrigonometry"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/tusi-nasir-al-din-bio" rel="nofollow">"ṬUSI, NAṢIR-AL-DIN i. Biography"</a>. <i>Encyclopaedia Iranica</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2018-08-05</span></span>. <q>His major contribution in mathematics (Nasr, 1996, pp. 208-214) is said to be in trigonometry, which for the first time was compiled by him as a new discipline in its own right. Spherical trigonometry also owes its development to his efforts, and this includes the concept of the six fundamental formulas for the solution of spherical right-angled triangles.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=%E1%B9%ACUSI%2C+NA%E1%B9%A2IR-AL-DIN+i.+Biography&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopaedia+Iranica&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iranicaonline.org%2Farticles%2Ftusi-nasir-al-din-bio&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATrigonometry"></span></span>
</li>
